		<fullReview>I honestly think 1997 was the best year for movies. Look at all the calibre hits that came out that year. Even the non-great films (The Edge, Spawn) were still cool to see.Out of everything that came out that year, The Ice Storm is the most overlooked. I had only heard of it after seeing Gene Siskel's annual Top 10 lists. He ranked this the best movie of 1997. Although I disagree that it is the best, it is certainly in my top 10.The Ice Storm stars Kevin Kline, Joan Allen, Sigourney Weaver, Henry Czerny, Tobey Maguire, Christina Ricci, Elijah Wood, Katie Holmes and David Krumholtz. Aside from Titanic, this has the best cast of 1997. The movie is about two neighbouring families: the Hoods (Kline, Allen, Maguire, Ricci) and the Carvers (Weaver, Sheridan, Wood, Byrd). Each member have their own subplots that are ruining their lives, and eventually intertwine. Kline is sleeping with Weaver and struggling with drinking; Allen suspects infidelity; Wood wants to get serious with Ricci but she wants to do some exploration; Maguire gets high with his prep friends and wants Holmes. The coldest storm ever is about to hit and effect everyone. This is a coming of age story for everyone in it. I could really relate to every subplot. Everybody is selfish and not caring about the other, that is pretty much what the movie is about. When the storm hits, some images stay with you as they symbolize different things in the story. Sometimes, ice is a nice thing to see and we also see it in its darkness. Each time the ice changes its mood, it foreshadows the mood coming up. The ice could also be used to represent how the main characters are: cold. None of the characters are really nice or likable. But they are still very compelling and interesting. How Ang Lee could do that is beyond me. They live in the woods in suburban New York. That could show how isolated they are with reality and the rest of the world. It all builds up to a shocking and heartfelt conclusion that stays with you for a long time. The denouement is a real tearjerker. Afterwards, you are left thinking about the movie as a whole and what it all says. Everything that the movie says is phenomenal.Still Ang Lee's best movie.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The Ice Storm paints a portrait of the average, Joe-blow American lifestyle. There's an affair. There's a wife who knows there is an affair going on with her husband. There's teenagers curious about it. There's many conversations revolving around sex between: adults to adults, teens to teens, adults to teens. In the 2009 film that's set in the late '60s, "Nine". Kate Hudson has a line saying that sexual revolution is coming to the surface. This film takes place a little after that and shows that it's true. The huge sexual revolution came upon everyday people who swore by God and the bible all their lives. They were confused. They decreased with social skills because of it. And they became so addicted to it. Thinking about it. Talking about it. Living by it. It's a very strong-messaged film. Besides the wonderful script, the acting is the strongest point. Joan Allen, Sigourney Weaver, Kevin Kline, and Christine Ricci in particular turn in excellent work here. It's one of the best ensembles of the '90s. The way, each actor can control a scene controlled by another actor is really interesting. While, it may drag at time with some of the less interesting characters (i.e. Tobey MaGuire, Elijah Wood), but still solid work from Ang Lee. James Schmaus fashions a script that really gets you thinking and discussing life. Lee handles the script in such a delicate way without manic scenes or over-the-top melodrama. This could've been a really dull film, but I don't know what it is that Lee does, but turns it around to make it non-boring, if that makes sense. You get to see Christine Ricci's best performance, as well as snubbed Oscar-worthy performances from Allen and Weaver; 8.5</fullReview>

		<username>fwomp</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Be Forewarned: The Pacing is Sloooooooow</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Ice Storm is a startling look at 1973 America and how lost so many people (parents, children, neighbors, etc.) became during this transitional time. The free-love of the 60s spill over into the 30 and 40-somethings of a sleepy Connecticut community and runs smack into the dysfunctional family of Ben and Elena Hood (Kevin Kline and Joan Allen).Ben is having an affair with his neighbors wife, Janey Carver (Sigourney Weaver, ALIENS), and the boredom they both feel during their "love making" is palpable. They want something different to happen, but what that "something" is remains unobtainable. And the two's lackadaisical attitudes toward sex, family, and the times seep into their everyday lives; even into their spouses' and children.Ben's wife Elena gets caught shoplifting from the local pharmacy. Janey's husband Jim goes away on long business trips and returns with funks of depression because of what he witnesses going on in his family.The children of Ben and Janey are also settling into this bizarre behavior. Some of them explore sexuality in often strange ways, or run out into storms and play on ice-covered roads. Others blow things up with M80 firecrackers, or smoke pot and drink to numb themselves out.But all of these people's lives will change in an unalterable way during one evening; an ice storm has hit and the cold outside bashes against the cold each character feels inside. Some will live, some will die, and others will never be the same.The attention to detail on the 70s in director Ang Lee's movie is astounding. The sweater-vest outfits, paneled station wagons, and boxy homes were excellently filmed.You can also feel the dark undercurrent running through the characters' lives: the grayish landscape cinematography against the quiet homes that harbor secrets.The cast was absolutely perfect, too. Kevin Kline (one of my favorite actors), Sigourney Weaver, Joan Allen, Elijah Wood, Tobey Maguire, Katie Holmes, Christina Ricci, and Jamey Sheridan all pull in stunning performances.Having gushed over how well done the movie was, I do have to comment on its pacing. If you thought SIDEWAYS was snail-like, this movie may make you feel as though grass could grow faster.Director Ang Lee has had some serious success in Hollywood as of late (BROKEBACK MOUNTAIN, 2005), but this earlier work is an excellent way to see how he's grown in the intervening years.</fullReview>

		<username>Matt Maisano</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A daring film that explores the negative sides of humanity. SPOILERS!!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Matt Maisano IDS 210 9/27/04Ang Lee's The Ice storm is a daring film with fresh, realistic characters. It is a story that we usually won't see at the multiplexes, and there are reasons for that. The Ice storm explores different views of family life than is traditionally shown on the silver screen, and it is a better film because of it. I will discuss the film first with a brief summary of the film's plot, then go into an examination of its content, in other words what I think meaning of the story is.The film takes place in New Canaan, a wealthy town in Fairfield County. The story for the most part follows two families, each with two children about high school age. The kids do drugs, have sex at a young age, and are overall confused and unguided by their parents. The parents engage in spousal trades and secret affairs. Tension builds until the closing of the film when Mikey gets electrocuted to death outside in the storm. Now lets back up and talk about morality and symbolism in The Ice stormAs I stated in the introduction, The Ice Storm is a film that presents its audience with perspectives of family life and adolescence that we rarely see in film. There is no sugarcoating in this movie. Take for example the dialogue in the film. Almost all of the characters speak in short, often cynical or 'cold' sentences. The filmmaker uses this sense of 'coldness' in his characters, and applies it to the environment of the film. If the kids are talking to their parents they say only what is necessary to response. Even in the thanksgiving scene, when Paul's family is all together at the dinner table, there was still an enormous sense of distance between everyone. The temperature of the film can be felt by the conversations and interactions of the characters within the story.Tobey Maguire's character Paul hood makes a quote in the film that I think strongly connects to this idea of sort of a raw, cold world in the film. He says something like 'Everyone exists in a negative zone that we dip in and out of. Some people get tempted by it and stay in the negative zone.' This line is sort of a summary of the film in itself. In almost all the interactions there is a sense of this lurking negativity that comes and goes from the scene. It is almost a character itself. To make a more specific example, Paul's father Ben is who I think the quote is supposed to symbolize most. He has dug himself a hole of negativity way before we even see him on screen. He's cheating on his wife and is detached from his family because of his lack of parenting. So we could make the connection that he is in the negative zone, well into it. When he tries to bond with his kids, and tries to connect with his wife, he is dipping into the 'positive zone' but never really staying there. As the film goes on we see his affair, and more importantly his marriage, become ruined. His temptations gave in to the lurking negativity and became consumed by it. In conclusion, The Ice Storm is an excellent film about families that are separated and alienated from each other. It's about confused adolescence, and at the same time the struggles of adulthood and marriage. The main aspects that I find most interesting about the film are the coldness of its characters and that relation to their environment, and the negativity that walks among them.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Well acted, strong message, dull as hell.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It takes a lot to make a film about adultery, wife swapping, underage sex, set during the height of the Watergate scandal anything less than completely engaging but Ang Lee has managed it here. The acting is astonishing. Kevin Kline, as usual, shows himself to be one of the most versatile and underused actors in the world, Sigouney Weaver adds another vampy M.I.L.F. to her repetoire, Christina Ricci makes you wonder what she will do when she gets to old to play Lolita's, and Elijah Wood makes up for being so irritating in the Lord of the Rings films. The problem lies with the direction. The film is so full of symbolic burts of nature (frosts, winds, trees billowing in threatening breezes) and stilted, narcotised, emotions (everyone in the film is chugging on downers throughout) that you begin to feel you are in an encounter group for depressed urban bhuddists. The main theme of the film, sexual repression and curiosity leading to ultimately tragic consequences, is made clear but it is not a film that you can actually enjoy. Although I think the later film is highly overrated this is a forerunner to rather than better than American Beauty.</fullReview>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Observational look at 70's suburban life. Solid and true to life, but not re-watchable.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Ice Storm is a dissection of suburban life with a `winter storm' climax, hence the title. It has a very large cast and for that reason it can't simply focus on one character. If one sub-plot reaches a climax they quickly switch over to another story, so it's observational but not participatory. Despite the fact that there are a number of different characters, they all manage to nicely develop. Kevin Kline shows his acting range here and does well as the sleazy, cheating husband. Given that he was in a lot of comedies that same year (Fierce Creatures, In and Out, Wild, Wild West) this is a pleasant surprise. In one of Joan Allen's breakthrough roles she also shows her acting depth and gives a very humane performance. Sigourney Weaver is delightful is a super b*tch. The youger actors are every bit as good in their roles aswell. A pre-Spiderman Tobey Maguire does well as the hormone driven son. Christina Ricci is also good as a 14-year-old seductress, as is a pre-Frodo Elijah Wood. The least known of the younger cast, Adam Hann Byrd is very impressive here. He already proved is acting ability in Little Man Tate as Jodie Foster's son and he is very convincing here. Too bad his career sort of vanished. The only person who didn't impress me though was Jamey Sheridan as Sigourney Weaver's husband. He only makes his first full appearance at about 20 minutes from the end and even then, he doesn't make much of an impact.The Ice Storm is very true to life in its vision of adult and adolescent life. The film does end a bit too suddenly but it proves the point that life doesn't stop after a tragedy. But still, they could have gone into a bit more detail there. Overall, The Ice Storm is a hugely impressive and muchly underrated film. It has some flaws but with a cast like this, what's on screen is never boring. 7.6 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>This is a very cold infernal family drama. It distracts us, so that we remain still, almost as if sleeping - and that gets you, in the winter.The actors are very skilled at making very convincing characters, but they, too, are just distractions. The ice is the main character, and no matter what people do or don't do to each other, it is the ice that makes the final move and ends the game.The old Greeks based the universe on the four elements - fire, earth, air and water - and indeed, these fundamental entities surround and penetrate our existence, to the extent that one can wonder how much they control it. In this movie, all that happens seems to be an unavoidable consequence of the weather - and it makes sense.Maybe we should conclude about the weather, like with so many other things, that it's all in our heads.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Dismemberment of the American dream</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Long before pretentious "American Beauty", this Ang Lee film excellently delivered us the dismemberment of American dream. Illegitimate affairs, drugs, depression, emotional alienation, all comprise the dark layout of American society, not only of the 90s but of the 70s, as well. Beneath the illustrious surface of the wealthy suburban civilians lurks nothing but dysfunctional, alienated families. Lee portrays all characters in a Chekov-like way, deepening in their emotions and thoughts. All actors offer us sensational performances: Kevin Kline, obsessed with his secret affair, Sigourney Weaver, icy and confused, Joan Allen, with no comprehenshion of the situation and the problematic adolescents, Maguire, Wood and Ricci. This movie is an undoubted masterpiece which was unjustly ignored by the Oscars.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>This ain't a feel good movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Contain SPOILERS!!!The lack of warmth and care these characters offer to each other is so graphically depicted that it's actually uncomfortable to view. Large parts of "The Ice Storm" feel like watching your hosts have a 'silent treatment' argument during your visit.Two fairly well-off families, Hoods and Carvers live in close proximity to each other in early '70s suburban New Haven, CT. Their living expenses are taken care of, as both husbands hold good-paying jobs, so they devote a lot of their time and energy to more unusual pursuits. They're family friends, attending snooty parties together and hanging in the same circle. Yet, that doesn't stop Ben Hood (Kevin Kline) from engaging in a sexual affair with Janey Carver (Sigourney Weaver). His wife Elena (Joan Allen) with whom he occasionally attends couples therapy suspects something is going on and has every proof short of catching them in the act, but seems already too much out of love to even bother properly confronting him. For their part, Ben and Janey are also just going through the motions - their love making is devoid of any emotion or excitement that they actually end up driving each other back to their original mates. After one of their encounters as they're laying in bed he starts telling her about his golf game and problems at work. "Ben, you're boring me" - she stops him - "I have a husband, I don't particularly feel a need for another". In cold exchanges like this one, and there's plenty of them between all characters, they demonstrate the full extent of their detachment from everything and everybody. All seem to be in constant search for something, but have no clue what, where and how.Hood offspring, 16-yr old stoner Paul (Tobey Maguire), who compared to what the other three kids are into looks the be the best adjusted one, is away at private high-school and his strange 14-yr old sister Wendy (Christina Ricci) along with Carver sons, a spaced-out flake of a kid Mikey (Elijah Wood) and silent militant Sandy (Adam Hann-Byrd) are all equally weird and screwed-up, younger versions of their parents.All parts are richly observed and really give the actors something to bite into - and they respond with fabulous performances. Kudos to director Ang Lee and writer James Schamus for successfully bringing the style, the issues and the atmosphere of this period. This is a powerful character study.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Icy weather, icy people</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love this movie. It balances pathos and humor as well as "American Beauty" did but adds the cultural references: Nixon, Vietnam, Steve Winwood and Traffic on the turntable, gaudy outfits, Devil Dogs, "modern" homes, Pringles potato chip canisters, etc. Although it examines two dysfunctional families, the movie is really about the gap between children and their elders in the early 70s, as apathetic a time as any I guess. It takes a tragedy at the end to bridge this gap once the ice stops falling. What is most surprising is that Ang Lee directed this very American story. Also surprising is Sigourney Weaver as the iciest of the characters. She has never been one of my favorite actors but her dialogue is some of the film's most memorable. Kevin Kline also turns in another fantastic performance, a forerunner to Kevin Spacey's Lester Burnham in American Beauty. In fact, the more I think about the actors in the film, the more I realize that everyone does a great job, right down to Jamey Sheridan (last seen as "Flagg" in the TV movie of "The Stand"), Katie Holmes (another TV vet), and Allison Janney. The atmosphere was thick with shock and surprise, no one moved, no one made a sound, everyone was so caught up in the film that all their attention was fixed upon that screen. I have never experienced any thing like it. If you like the more independent style films then you absolutely must see this one. You will not regret it. All the acting is stunning (and i would include Tobey Maguire as well) and the film is beautiful. Nothing else will touch you, or affect you like this. Please share in my unforgetable experience.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>a wonderful movie, but a bit maudlin</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The last person mentioned that the 'ice storm' might be emblematic of something, but wasn't sure what...I think it is an appropriate image for the sexual revolution (and the political turmoil of the late 1960s in general): while it is occurring it seems dramatic and violent, but what it ultimately produces is just ice. The movie seems to be about just that: while the sexual revolution promised freedom, it may have produced dysfunctionality instead. Ice is coldness -- the opposite of intimacy. I have heard someone else characterize the theme this way: freedom is great until participation becomes mandatory. Then it is just compulsion again (think of the 'car key party' scene). So as far as I am concerned, the movie is about the compulsions that freedom can produce.</fullReview>

		<username>yaseminturkish</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A new perspective on the family genre but the characters needed more personality</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Who would have thought that Ang Lee known most for the somewhat overrated but stellar film Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon would provide an engrossing new take on the American family genre? Though The Ice Storm was a bit too cold for my taste and some of the characters seemed as though their personalities were frozen on ice for 100 years, this piece was a moving look at the family genre that has yet to be fully explored.  Inevitably I think, The Ice Storm will be compared with American Beauty, Terms of Endearment, and Ordinary People and we will all ponder how it stacks up against the competition. I would put it somewhere in the middle. American Beauty revealed to the viewer characters that had a lot of fire and charisma and their personalities were often strange but engaging. The flip side of the coin remains that Annette Bening often over-acted the role and Kevin Spacey was at the top of his game, though his odd speeches and mannerisms didn't fit with the typical American father making American Beauty virtually impossible to fully appreciate.  There has never been another film quite like the family genre miracle Ordinary People. Though it was released in 1980 years before The Ice Storm, Terms of Endearment, and American Beauty it has not aged a bit. I was recently trying to remember an Oscar-worthy performance with a female that could put to shame Meryl Streep's Sophie's Choice and Vivien Leigh's Gone with the Wind and only Mary Tyler Moore as the coldest mother on Earth who plays favorites came to mind. The role was difficult because Moore's character had to be subtle but opinionated at the same time. She effortlessly conveyed with her eyes and heart what her mouth couldn't tell Timothy Hutton. And Donald Sutherland reminds me so much of my own father and the typical American father (a tax attorney who cares but knows when to let his child go his own way) it was uncanny. Ordinary People is a better film than American Beauty and The Ice Storm because the characters remind us of real mothers and fathers. The Ice Storm was filled with characters that were a bit too subdued. Did Joan Allen take Prozac to prepare for the role? Christina Ricci got it right and appeared to be emulating Thora Birch in American Beauty but Tobey McGuire seemed to be in the wrong movie. Timothy Hutton was meant for his Ice Storm role. Kevin Kline was dependable, but the script refused to allow us to fully comprehend who the characters were. They seemed fairly disengaged from each other and most importantly from us. That said, The Ice Storm is a memorable film that was unfortunately less seen and applauded than it's sub-par counterpart American Beauty. The ice storm was quite real, the thrill elements exciting, and most specifically the feeling that it was all leading up to something big engaging. If there was no Ordinary People to compare it to, The Ice Storm would remain the best in the family genre and a 10/10. Oh well.9/10</fullReview>
	</review>

Basically the Ice Storm is a griping tale of two American families and how they are somewhat destroyed, all in the beautiful setting of a winter storm. Ang Lee never lets the viewer forget that serene winter atmosphere, as he beautifully films ice-covered, leafless trees, trains struggling to move through the frozen power lines with icicles hanging from it's rusted metal wheels, and the frosted and slippery streets and forest trails. This setting will become a significant factor in the film; a factor that proves the setting could be no other way. Firstly, the acting was suburb by the entire cast (Joan Allen, Tobey Maguire, Kevin Kline, Sigourney Weaver, Elijah Wood, and Christina Ricci). The cinematography was flawless, and along with the beautiful camera shots, the movie was visually breathtaking. The plot was quite good, but the real key factor in making this film great is how the actors presented emotions on-screen. In summation, this was an incredible film, one of my favorites, and is a real reason why the unique Ang Lee is one of the best modern directors.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Better than American Beauty</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Although it never received the recognition it deserved, this film was a triumph for Ang Lee. I thought it was better than Lee's `Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon', which was novel for someone who had never seen a Hong Kong film before, but certainly not totally original for those who have. `The Ice Storm' is also better than the film with the exact same theme that came two years later, `American Beauty'. I can understand why `Beauty' was more popular though, because it was ?hip', less dark, and more immediately satiating. While it was a good film in its own right, I still found the `The Ice Storm' to be much more graceful, and lacking the predictability of `Beauty'. It was quiet, intense and extremely intelligent-- perhaps too much so for today's audience, but a treat to those who still appreciate subtlety in filmmaking. `The Ice Storm' is by no means the first of its kind to touch upon the disenchantment of American ideals, as `The Graduate' did it thirty years earlier. It is, however, a gem of a film, and one that I hope will not soon be forgotten.</fullReview>

		<date>2001-12-19</date>
		<username>Vince-5</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A penetrating chill</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Typically, when a movie takes place in a fairly recent time period, the filmmakers do one of two things--A: Romanticize the period, or B: Turn it into a jokey cartoon caricature of what it was. The latter is especially true when the period in question is the 1960s or '70s. This era is consistently portrayed as some sort of avocado-green polyester nightmare (as opposed to the boringly-neutral-beige combed-cotton nightmare of present society). The Ice Storm, however, takes neither approach. While I won't say that the time is incidental, we are not pummeled with it; in fact, we can even forget it after a while. The film is about (surprise!) people.The place is New Canaan, Connecticut; the time, winter 1973. The people are a group of alienated, sexually repressed/frustrated/bored characters. All want different things, but all are united by their desperate longing for personal satisfaction. They mix, discussing Watergate, Deep Throat, and Styrofoam packing peanuts, letting their own issues simmer without a word. In private, they all struggle to break out of their numbing suburban routine. But numbing though it may be, they really don't know anything else. So where do they go?Ang Lee has created the type of film that is, sadly, an anomaly in Hollywood: A picture that makes you think. The characters are merely observed, without judgment, revealing their personalities, fears, aches, longings. Lee never goes out of his way to make them sympathetic; whether you care about any of them or not all depends on your own personality, fears, aches, longings. The interactions of these incompatible people, together yet isolated, are never less than fascinating.The film is marvelously put together. The photography is beautiful, combining with great sets and costumes for the perfect atmosphere. We see a lot of ice--in trays, on branches, etc--and though the metaphor may not be subtle, it works perfectly. Though the soundtrack includes some great pop tunes, they are merely background music, heard only when characters put them on. The acting, which is the true backbone of the movie, is fantastic (though I don't care for Katie Holmes). Stand-outs include Christina Ricci as a teenager experimenting with sex (it happens--get over it), and polar-opposite wives Joan Allen and Sigourney Weaver (who has never looked more beautiful). This is a dark, barren winter landscape worthy of exploration. Expect to be chilled...but leave your coat in the closet.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A truly wonderful film!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Having just seen 'The Ice Storm' for the first time on VHS, I felt compelled to write my opinion of it. Firstly, my faith in Ang Lee has been restored, after the over-rated, convoluted mish-mash of 'CROUCHING TIGER, HIDDEN DRAGON' (2000), a ghastly and dull experience for me.But enough about that film. This is one of the greatest experiences I've had in the cinema since 'AMERICAN BEAUTY' (1999), (forgive this flash-forwarding as if it's flashbacking; I never saw 'ICE...' at the cinema.) Kevin Kline delivers a subtle and nuanced performance, the likes of which he has never given before. Sigourney Weaver puts her icy disposition to good use in her scenes with Kline, and I couldn't help but think back to their marvellous chemistry in the movie 'DAVE' (1995)The children deliver wonderful performances; the always reliable Christina Ricci is heart rending as a young girl confused about sexuality; the often problematic Tobey Maguire wonderful as her brother, confused and unsure of his feelings, particularly towards fellow classmate Libbets Casey (Katie Holmes). Holmes' performance is also outstanding; I was never a fan of the TV show 'DAWSON'S CREEK,' and was never impressed with her 'performance' in that show, but with this and two other films, ("Go" (1999) and "WONDER BOYS" (2000)) she proves she is a subtle and charismatic actress, fast emerging as one of my favourites.Elijah Wood and Adam Hann-Byrd are outstanding as Weaver's sons; their scenes together play as natural; they are real brothers with real rivalries and Hann-Byrd ('LITTLE MAN TATE' (1991)) again proves what a wonderous talent he is.But the film is held together by Kline, who wanders through the wonderfully hideous 70's stylings, bringing the story to its heart-breaking conclusion, which I defy anyone to stay dry eyed through. This film gets a definite 10 out of 10.</fullReview>

As a result, Ben Hood (Kevin Cleine) starts and affair with Janey (Sigourney Weaver). Their children are at the verge of entering puberty. Ben's only daughter, very promiscuous Wendy, makes out with both brothers of Carver family, sons of Janey and Mike Carver. Then he speaks of this event to his wife with a lame excuse for his presence in Janey's house causing his wife Elena (Joan Allen) to suspect that he is having an affair. A very young Tobey Maguire, Paul Hood, brother of promiscuous Wendy Hood, is deeply in love with Libbet Casey, who seems sweet to Paul but in fact she is pretentious and immature. There are three main stories in the movie. 1) Affair of Ben and Janey and the following events after Ben's wife Elena (Joan Allen) finds out about the affair, 2) Wendy and her relationship to two brothers, 3) Paul's relationship to Libbet which has little room in the entire picture compared to first two stories. It is claimed that events take a turn after the ice storm but in fact they take a turn after Elena finds about the affair of her husband. Ice Storm was generally a very successful picture but I have one critique to make. The Ice Storm was not really ice storm at all. I expected to see more Ice and Snow because that is expression given in the movie. However, scenes related to Ice Storm were poorly made. For example in the scene where Elena and Jim Carver are walking towards the car, they slide slightly due to the supposedly icy ground but there is almost no ice on the ground. In fact, you can see that it is raining. Then they reach the car whose doors and windows are frozen. How can there be a rain and ice storm at the same time? If there is rain, then the temperature is not low enough to create ice. You only see frozen tree branches but that's the only ice you see in the movie. This is a very small detail and it doesn't ruin the story but somehow it bothered me.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>a little snapshot of suburbia in 1973</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After seeing 'the Ice Storm' several times I have decided it's one of my all-time favorites. When a film captivates me the way this one does, I have to love it even if the subject matter is disturbing. The characters are unsympathetic and even unlikable, but you just can't stop watching them get further entangled and deeper into trouble. The parents and the teenagers walk around in a numbed haze of 70's suburban boredom until a harsh reality wakes everyone from their slumber. Kevin Kline, Joan Allen, and Sigourney Weaver are convincing as 50's generation parents desperately trying to adjust to the sexual revolution of the 70's. Kevin Kline plays a middle-aged executive who goes temporarily insane with the free-love standards of the times. He truly loves his wife and kids but becomes recklessly involved with a neighbor's frigid wife, played by Sigourney Weaver. Joan Allen plays his repressed 'Donna Reed-ish' wife, who makes Thanksgiving dinner look lovely even though her life has become very ugly. Christina Ricci plays their sexually-curious daughter, who is way too interested in her boyfriend's little brother. Their goofy, nerdy son is played by Tobey Maguire, who ironically resembles a young Kevin Kline. The 1973-era nostalgia is great to watch for. I especially enjoyed the Jim Croce song "I Got a Name" which is skipping on an old vinyl-LP. The final scene leaves the viewer grasping for a resolution where there was never meant to be one. So what if it doesn't have an earth-shattering point to make, so what if the characters are miserable, so what if it doesn't have a happy ending...that's how real life is!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>fantastic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Ice Storm is a wonderful, character driven picture that not only documents upper middle class reactions to the changing morals of the 1970's but also reflects these changes on the actions of their children. Ang Lee's film perfectly captures the New Cannan/Greenwich/Bedford moral and physical landscape. Boarding schools, bongs, bored housewives. The way that the 1970s pop references ( Deep Throat, EST, waterbeds ) are woven into the story that illuminate Lee's themes of social change, WASP repression and juvenile exploration. All the acting is wonderful (except Kevin Kline's routine descent into physical comedy that he seems to put everywhere). Sigourney Weaver gives a wonderful performance as a jaded, feral housewife who damages people and relationships as easily as her youngest son destroys toys. Christina Ricci is amazing as a sexually precocious young teen who acts like the bad girl yet only wants "to touch it". Elijah Wood is brilliant as a spacehead who is obsessed with molecules to the incomprehension of all. There are touching moments that bring tears ( the tragic end, Kline carrying Ricci over the wet ground ) that evoke a family in crisis with itself and the times but their emotional core is still solid. American Beauty got all the attention from Oscar but The Ice Storm is as far as it can be from that satire cum tragedy. Very complex script, wonderful acting make this movie worth repeated viewing. In my opinion a great movie.</fullReview>

That won't change anything."Cause our life (okay, my life) has as few meaning as life of those people, young and old in "Ice Storm". Like AB it's a film for all the family (well everyone aged 14 and over) - it's a very moral film with an important story to tell. 		<username>James.S.Davies</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Sensitive 70s Sensibilities</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Like his previous film Sense and Sensibility, Ang Lee's latest intense character-based drama is a period piece. This one is rather more contemporary however, as it's set in 1970s upstate New York rather than 18th century rural England. Once again he demonstrates his remarkable versatility, not only in setting but in the fact that the film's dramatic qualities is often matched by a wonderful subtle humour.The Ice Storm essentially portrays how the liberal sensibilities of the parents in the film disrupted the up-bringing of their off-spring. The young teenagers seem almost forced in to their sexual awakenings. The extra-marital social practices of the parents makes them problematic role models. Despite the supposed sexual liberation the emotions in this film seem just as repressive as they did in Austen's England. The emotions of the serial adultress played by Sigourney Weaver seem as cold as the storm itself. Even Kline's emotions are only eventually thawed out through a mixture of drunken jealousy and grief.Comparisons are now bound to be made with 'American Beauty'. It certainly isn't as accessible as that already proclaimed classic, but its possibly a more rewarding film. The cast are all notable for the realism they bring in their portrayals of the dysfunctional 70s characters. While Lee's direction is real quality, with the imagery he brings to the screen being one of The Ice Storm's most commendable qualities.</fullReview>

Surely the novel by Rick Moody must be better. I recommend this film to older people who had similar experiences during the 70's, and people in general who enjoy this kind of real-life drama. However, if you're like me and expect something fantastic, insightful, special or just plain fun in a film, go somewhere else. 7/10</fullReview>

Like American Beauty, The Ice Storm focuses on a nuclear family with problems, however, the approach in Ang Lee's film is more subtle. You don't see children conversing about "offing" their parents without any real motivation. But you do sense the existence of an underlying pain that begins to float to surface through a series of actions brought about by the characters, and finally surfaces at the film's finale when the unthinkable has happened. The interesting thing is that, the characters in this film, aren't baffled by the event in the film's final scenes. The event is an awakening for the characters and quite cathartic.</fullReview>

		<date>2000-02-22</date>
		<username>evanharvey99</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Me-Decade Morality Play</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'll put a fine point on it: The Ice Storm is a comedy. Yes, its humour is so subdued and well modulated that hardly anyone finds it funny. Yes, the story features an undeniably tragic turn during the last third. Yes, the entire film seems somber and slow, perhaps even (literally and figuratively) coloruless. So is it a clever exercise in genre subversion, black comedy taken so far that the laughs are stripped bare? Hardly. The Ice Storm is comedic because it reverses our expectations as an audience (and as Americans in particular). If you could only attune yourself to its very delicate pitch, the laughter is audible.This is not a wry parody of the stylistic and cultural foibles of 1970s America, though that's how it may play from time to time and many people expected as much going in. Instead, director Ang Lee (and Rick Moody, who wrote the original novel) sets up a series of moral challenges in order to explore the sexual politics of that much-maligned decade. On one level, yes, we can say that this is true. The husband (Kevin Kline, who finds the right tone for an underdeveloped character) confronts his infidelity; the wife Joan Allen, spot-on) does likewise; their faux-naif daughter (Christina Ricci) wrestles, in mirror-like fashion, with the same issues. Their son (Tobey Maguire) separates himself from the family circus, both physically and emotionally, but is eventually confronted with his own moral dilemma. And contrary to all of our expectations, he is the only one who chooses wisely.We want a swinging suburban expose and are given behavioral object lessons; we want vibrant leisure suits onscreen and pulsating disco music on the soundtrack, but are given the most discrete visual and aural tableaux; we want to make plain the conflicted American sexual personae, but are given obfuscation and layer upon layer of inarticulate opinion. The Ice Storm knows what we want and refuses, by the very power of its form, to give in to us. We are teased by the film, frustrated, unsure of how to react or if we should even care. Life is more often than not a simple moral choice: do this thing or do that thing. Sex does not complicate the issue, contrary to public doctrine, but simplifies it. And that's pretty funny when you think about it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>1999-11-18</date>
		<username>TomC-5</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>a mood piece with a fine ensemble cast</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What some may see as a flawed film - i.e., boring, pointless, flat, or offering unsympathetic characters, Ang Lee's THE ICE STORM is actually a very well acted slice of Americana - that is, Americana in its suburban Connecticut, upper-middle class mid 70s incarnation. Not an epic story, with a complex plot, this movie is more what I would call a mood piece. And the predominant mood is one of sadness and pathos, as those depicted here continuously fail to connect with one another on anything but the most superficial levels. Now one might ask: is this reality? Is this an accurate characterization of a sudden shift in the culture at this time. Well, no, not really. of course, this is drama, right, not anthropology and the director, actors and writer are entitled to poetic license. Here this license is put to good effect.The other key point here is that the narrator is a juvenile, and he has a bit of Holden Caulfield in him (just a bit, that is). In other words, he feels disconnected from the adult world in, in normal adolescent ways, ways he doesn't fully understand. The movie plays off of this theme of the disconnect between children and adults; my favorite instance of this was Janey's (Sigourney Weaver) ludicrous pseudo-intellectual utterances while chastising of Wendy (Christina Ricci) for having just more or less terrified her young son Sandy by leading him in a game of doctor. In depicting a world of those who are young and psychologically adrift, in spite of seemingly having all the privileges one could possibly want, it captures something quite real. Having grown up in the same era, I can also say there were some subtle moments here which felt quite real.I would rate this rather high, then: 9 out of 10, particularly for some wonderful performances by Weaver, Allen, Ricci and Wood</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Dysfunctional people dysfunction in dysfunctional 1970s US</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>God knows where cinema would be without Richard Nixon. Looking for a handy visual metaphor for what's wrong / right / good / bad and hey, let's turn on the television, there's ol' Tricky Dicky spouting off.Dicky's in Ice Storm, natch, because here's a study of microcosmic America slowly fragmenting. Like, er, ice. It's the 1970s, of course, and the height of Watergate (of course) so obviously everything's going to eventually splinter. Like, um, ice.So's everyone, too: adults, children, the entire population of New Canaan seems at this time to be ready to snap / fracture / splinter. Reason? They're all dysfunctional. Requote that: they're all abnormal. They're all overdressed 1970s mannequins in overstyled 1970s sets and they're all pretty much over wrought plot devices. (Bit like Tricky Dicky, really.)Of course, there is a point to The Ice Storm. It's about how dysfunctional people are miserable. Even the guy who's working on developing silicon (get it? 1970s? Verge of the great chip break-through? Wow, this period feel is just so authentic!) is miserable. (Worse, even Sigourney Weaver is miserable, but then anybody would be considering the make-up and costume inflicted on her).Quite what Ang Lee and a fairly good cast (though Kline sleepwalks through it other than in those moments when he's not raising his eyebrows) thought they were doing with a clunker like this is hard to fathom. The film has nothing to say and spends far too much time saying it.As to the storm, it's about as relevant to the plot as a hurricane or monsoon: anything could have been dragged screaming into the end to fill up the space, and arguably to greater dramatic effect -- storm, what storm? A few drops of rain. A few sprayed on special effect trees. Some forlorn attempts to get the characters' breaths to cloud on air that so evidently isn't freezing.America, one is delighted to say, wasn't like this and ain't like this -- which means the film as metaphor (which has to be the only reason why it was ever made) fails from the outset.Not only does it fail, it fails in most portentous style, with one of the characters reciting gibberish from some pulp magazine or other, all about open doors and walking in unknowingly, a level of intellectualising bettered in any first grade school yard.Ice Storm? Slight breeze, one would suggest. And chilling only in that, after viewing it, you realise how much time you've wasted on such gloss 'n dross.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Good cast, interesting story, pretty scenery, slowly presented.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I first saw "The Ice Storm" in theaters, I was guided by both positive word of mouth and good reviews, and I concluded that, while it wasn't the best movie I'd ever seen, it was really pretty good.First, there's the cast, led by two of our best contemporary actors, Kevin Kline and Sigourney Weaver, both of whom give solid, believeable performances as the two essentially self-absorbed extramarital lovers. Then, there are the young people involved. Christina Ricci and Elijah Wood are probably two of the best teen actors we have today. If there are any two candidates for solid adult careers out there, it is they. And they deliver solidly as the uncertain Kat and the deeply troubled Mikey, respectively. But don't leave Adam Hann-Byrd out. As Sandy, Mikey's younger brother with an enormous crush on Kat, he's totally believeable. And, yes, he gets lucky by the end of the picture!But the outstanding scene, to me, was the "Key Party" scene. A big fad in the early '70's, those dear, dead, pre-AIDS days, it worked like this: Couples arriving at a party were asked, on a "strictly voluntary" basis, to drop their car keys in a bowl. Later in the evening, all the women present closed their eyes and picked one set of car keys out of the bowl. Then they would spend the night with whoever owned the keys, their husband/boyfriend or not. One couple, Kline and Weaver's respective spouses, couldn't even wait until they got home. There they were, in the front seat of the car, at the height of the storm, going at it like maniacs, with their feet sticking out the window!!!!!!! Ah, the '70's. Nothin' like 'em.My only problem, and it's a minor one, is one of historical accuracy. I grew up in Westchester County, New York, during this same period. And all I could think was: Where was I during all this? Were everybody's lives as stultifyingly normal as mine and my parents'? Apparently, the filmmakers thought not, and the result is a somewhat one-sided story.But don't let this discourage you. "The Ice Storm" really is a good film. It starts slow, but stick with it, and you won't be disappointed.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-04-15</date>
		<username>James Chong (chongj@worldnet.att.net)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A Necessary Evil</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Ice Storm" is a film that is so layered with symbolic meaning that it demands multiple viewings. Otherwise, it would be much too easy to brush off the film as a cold, depressing, and pointlessly bleak portrait of the lives it depicts.On one level, Ang Lee's film is about two upper-middle class New England families--the Hoods and the Carvers--and how they are forever impacted by the sexual revolution of the 1970s. On another level, this film concerns the healthy balance that Nature provides and how subverting this balance can lead to destructive, and even tragic, consequences.In both the Hood family and the Carver family, the parents essentially behave like children, while the children, who have no reliable (or respectable) authority figures to supervise them, stumble precariously into the arena of adult sexual awakening. Ben Hood (Kevin Kline) and Janey Carver (Sigourney Weaver) engage in an extramarital affair with one another because their spouses do not sexually satisfy them; Ben's wife Elena (Joan Allen) is frigid and Janey's husband Jim (Jamey Sheridan) is impotent. Essentially, Ben and Janey are so engulfed in their own selfish wants that they are oblivious to the potentially destructive consequences of their actions on their families.But their spouses are certainly not without foibles of their own. Elena Hood suffers from a mid-life crisis of sorts that leaves her longing to be free of adult responsibilities. In one scene, Elena watches wistfully as her daughter Wendy (Christina Ricci) rides past her on her bicycle, and a bit later we see Elena herself ride a bicycle into town and get caught shoplifting, which, incidentally, is a petty crime that her daughter is also involved in. And then there is Jim Carver--the clueless, mad scientist type who is so obsessed with his work that he has never learned to communicate with his own children. When he asks his sons Mikey (Elijah Wood) and Sandy (Adam Hann-Byrd) how school is going, the boys are so puzzled by what should be a mundane inquiry that they cannot give a coherent response.Indeed, failure of communication is a theme that is prevalent throughout "The Ice Storm." Just as Jim cannot talk directly to his children, so Ben fails to do so with his own. There is a notable scene in a car between Ben and his son Paul (Tobey Maguire) in which Ben awkwardly attempts to broach the subject of masturbation. It is obvious to everyone but Ben that Paul is at a stage of adolescence where he would be fully aware of such issues. The puzzled look on Paul's face confirms his own astonishment at his father's cluelessness. Similarly, when Janey Sheridan scolds Wendy Hood for sexually coming on to her younger son Sandy, she does so not through a forceful reprimand but by spouting out obscure, irrelevant facts about Eastern cultural traditions.What becomes painfully clear as "The Ice Storm" progresses is that the lives of these two families are slowly but surely spiraling out of control, and someone is ultimately going to have to pay for these sins. The time of reckoning finally arrives on the night that both the Hood and the Carver adults converge on a key party while a fierce ice storm strikes the area. The key party--in which the husbands toss their keys in a jar and the wives sleep with the men whose keys they randomly fish out--marks the lowest possible point of moral abandonment by the Hoods and the Carvers. Director Ang Lee effectively intercuts these crucial scenes to reveal that as the party progresses inside, so the ice storm rages outside--as if to vent Nature's fury at the chaos that man consistently chooses to embrace over the natural order.When the storm finally clears, and the effect of the alcohol wears off, what rises with the morning mist is a tragic revelation that wrenches all characters from their careless moral and spiritual slumbers. This is a devastating finale, and what is even more heartbreaking is the silent understanding that, for these two families, tragedy was clearly a necessary evil.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Stu-5</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A chilling, confronting and altogether well-made expose on American family in the 1970's</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When one of the characters breaks down and cries, we realise that the people we are seeing in The Ice Storm have no direction. Their lives comprise of wandering aimlessly through a haze of extramarital affairs, experimentation with drugs and sex and taking everything they have for granted. The Ice Storm shows how children mimic their parents, one character is portrayed brilliantly by Christina Ricci, who plays a young nymphomaniac obsessed with sex. She is a teenager in an uncertain time, and we eventually realise that the children in the movie have more direction and more control over their lives than the adults, who hasten to reprimand the kids for things they otherwise do.It's 1973 in America and two upper class families collide head on in a tale that illustrates American family with so much integrity that you'd swear it's a Robert Altman piece. It's told by Ang Lee, a Chinese man who immigrated to America, and it's his perspective of family back then. The Ice Storm delves into how people were quick to adulterate because it was the thing to do, and the sordid key parties, where woman fish car keys out of a bowl and sleep with other wives' husbands.Each of the characters in the movie are played brilliantly, notably by Kevin Kline who delivers a portrayal of a man with no control over his life. He has two kids of whom he rarely sees, a wife whom he's cheating on and a life that is utterly perfuncatory. Also, there's the WASP vixen played by Sigourney Weaver, whose numerous affairs result in her totally losing control over her family. There's the kids who are gradually learning the in's and out's of life like sex and drugs. Lastly, there's the teenager who narrates the story: he has control over his life, and it takes one who is in control to tell a story about people out of control. The two balance perfectly.All of these problems come face to face in an extremely confronting denouement, which results in each of the characters to analyse their own lives. It's a really sad story. We have pity for these characters.The climax is amplified and emphasised by the ice storm which hits where they live. Ang Lee captures each icicle which dangles from the tea leaves; the slick roads; the chilly air. It's shown with such precision and such care that it heightens the emotional impact in a film that was already charged with it anyway. It's a deep film that requires much concentration and thought.Each of the performances are brilliant, the script is first class, the impact is phenomenal and the visual side is breathtaking.Ten out of ten.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>If you look at it vaguely, "The Ice Storm" has absolutely no plot. But that`s what makes it so interesting. 		<username>Sean Gallagher (seankgallagher@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Powerful behavior study</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It seems to me that while the 60's were a time of clarity - you were either on one side or the other - the 70's were a time of confusion, at least for suburbanites, for whom the 60's mores(like the sexual revolution) were finally catching up to them, and they didn't know what to do about it. For me, that was one of the main points of the original novel by Rick Moody, and this powerful film, this feeling of confusion and being adrift, because no one knew what the rules were.This is perfect territory for Ang Lee, because this is a film less about what people say than how they act, and all of his previous films have been studies of human behavior. From the line for the train, where everyone male is wearing the same trenchcoat, to the key party, we are allowed to see the internal effects on each person of their behavior, even if they themselves can't see it. Lee is elliptical in his approach, which does take some getting used to, but his compassion is obvious. And all the performances are excellent, especially Ricci, who shows she's going to be a big star someday.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Were the 70's Really that Bad?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Fascinating, how the culture's global assessment of the '70s seems to get more negative from year to year. As someone old enough to remember, I felt this film caught beautifully the special quality of the alienation between parents and children in those days; the anger of middle-aged women caught in old-style marriages that suddenly weren't working and the confusion of middle class men who were working hard to pay the bills, the way their fathers had told them they should, but were suddenly confronted with wives who wouldn't sleep with them anymore (or were sleeping with the neighbors) and children who wouldn't talk to them.I think though, that with all the "reassessing" that's going on, there is a real danger that the tremendous energy and sheer frolicsome exuberance of those times may be forgotten. It's true that my family and nearly all my friends' families were more or less dysfunctional then. Still, I had a hell of a time. If I had a choice, I'd rather be depressed and lonely in the '70s when I could console myself by going to a friendly neighborhood orgy or anti-war demonstration (pick your poison) than in the '90s when people just lock themselves up at home and take Prozac until the darkness recedes sufficiently that they can face their computer screens again..</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Bleakness with bliss</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sometimes going to the movies is depressing - especially when the subject matter portrayed on the screen is less than joyous. Such is the case, especially, with Independent-type art house films like "The Ice Storm." While it features an all-star cast (Kevin Kline, Sigourney Weaver, Elijah Wood, Christina Ricci), the plot is subdued, virtually non-existent, as it starts in the middle of these people's lives and ends in the middle as well. It's the story of families, dysfunctional just as it was becoming trendy in the 1970's, who share nothing with each other, but heartache and cold emotionless relationships with everyone. Each character shows little care or concern for one another - only a blank love-void existence. The 70 's were a decade of experimentation with drugs, sex, and alcohol - and everyone, children included, are involved in this exploration.The performances put in by the children outshine their more famous adult co -stars, and that's good. Because it's easy for adults to play emotionless characters - they get plenty of practice. But for child actors to extinguish their natural emotions for a life completely void of feelings is difficult. Sigourney Weaver received a Golden Globe nomination, but I would rather have seen Christian Ricci recognized for her performance. She has a long career ahead of her.This movie is not a pleasant film experience - instead it serves as a warning, or a lesson, or maybe just a look at life and how it is. But it is a look at life being played out in tragic ways - not because of the tragedy that occurs towards the end of the film, but the tragedy of a family without love, and a life without emotion.</fullReview>

This is a shame (and a great loss to the casual moviegoer), because the insights provided in this awe -inspiring tribute to the mutation of the clueless upper-class New England social circles in the early 70s are not to be missed, and certainly not to be taken lightly. Kevin Kline and Joan Allen star as the unfaithful husband and tortured housewife that we are all so familiar with, though more through personal experience than anything else. First-rate supporting performances are turned in by Christina Ricci and Tobey Maguire as their children, and a nicely apathetic Sigourney Weaver as the mother whom Kline cheats with. There is so much perfectly leveled dialogue full of poignancy and irony in every scene; Ang Lee and so many others deserved Oscars for their work on this breathtaking film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>null</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Ice Storm is a brilliantly funny, satirical look at the fallout of the sexual revolution and its effect on an upper-class community, two neighboring families in particular. It presents the early seventies as a time of cultural confusion, with its "modern" trappings now appearing strangely foreign and often utterly silly. There is not a false line of dialogue nor a throw-away scene in the film, and the performances are uniformly excellent. Ang Lee has crafted this film with a startling eye for nuance that perhaps could only be beheld by an outsider. Kevin Kline and Christina Ricci are stand-outs, as the dense father/inconsiderate husband and his rapidly (if unevenly) maturing daughter. I have seen this movie five times in the last two months and look forward to watching it again. In fact, any time in the future that I may flirt with the notion of going to see a movie like Armageddon or Godzilla, one without a shred of imagination or respect for its audience , I will instead go back and view The Ice Storm once more. Repeated viewings of this one are far more rewarding than to waste even a minute watching the aforementioned and their like.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Look, I think Ang Lee is a fine director, but this film misses the mark despite aiming for the right target.Lee effectively uses a melancholy, wintry backdrop -- along with the Watergate scandal -- in his attempt to convey the post-'60s disillusionment that existed in the bleak and almost-cozily forlorn early-'70s...Almost.Unfortunately, the wonderful opportunity to capture this now-distant era wound up being reduced to a cliché'd cartoon. 		<date>2009-12-25</date>
		<username>tieman64</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Suburban Blues</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"There is a malaise that exists in your land ? what appears to many as the sudden and tragic disappearance of the American dream which, in some ways, has turned to nightmare." ? John Greene Part of a group of films ("Magnolia", "Safe", "American Beauty", "Fight Club", "Happiness", "Synecdoche NY", "Revolutionary Road", "Little Children" etc) released in the decade after 1995, all of which presented middle class American life as a suicidally dull bore, Ang Lee's "The Ice Storm" revolves around a small cast of affluent but disaffected characters.Most of the aforementioned pictures do the same things; they see the excesses of the 1980s ? a decade of materialistic pursuit and economic boom - giving way to a "comfortable capitalism" in possession of a suffocating underside. Forget the lower and middle classes. These films offered their audiences an array of wealthy characters, all of whom are subjected to a kind of pampered drudgery (the vacuity of materialism, of 9 to 5 routines, of cultural overload, of over stimulation etc). In almost all cases, these characters cope by resorting to violence, sex, drugs and/or fantasy. Such coping mechanisms, or addictions, always fail; in these films existential nausea knows no escape. All these films also fail to conjure up any new languages of despair (Antonioni, Bergman etc) and all seem blissfully unaware of the socio-psycho-economic causes of their ailments ("Eyes Wide Shut" would come along and rectify this).And like most of Woody Allen's work, these films also understand their character's problems solely through the lens of sexual disenchantment. They're populated by emasculated characters who see their plights as being a result of unfulfilled sexual desires. In other words, these films reduce happiness to biology. A lack of orgasms. A glitch involving neurochemicals. Indeed, Todd Solondz's "Happiness" liberalises this by ending with a kid ejaculating on his front porch. Few of these films recognise that desire is always buffeted by larger forces, and even fewer broaden their scope beyond the bedroom.7.5/10 ? Slickly shot and entertaining, this did what Sam Mendez did with "American Beauty" in a much less pretentious manner. Unfortunately, like most films in this cycle, it amounts to a kind of shallow existentialism. Worth one viewing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Under The Storm</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE ICE STORM **** Rick Moody's screenplay seems somewhat pretentious and unstable because its themes (marital issue, promiscuity, adolescence) aren't explored very deeply before it goes on to the next one - making it seem as though all he really wants is to give his characters emotional charge, or lack thereof - and not ending with quite the resolution it needed. However, the basic gist of the story still penetrates through as the film establishes itself, about life in 1973 Conneticut, where the barriers and rifts that form, amoral events that take place, and new facets of life being explored between all of the characters effect each of them differently. All of the events take place over the course of a weekend as an ice storm forms. Rick Moody and Ang Lee, a gifted director, work well together to coat 'The Ice Storm' with plenty of creative elements of film technique, but there just isn't much solid backbone to base it on. Four out of five stars.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-04-23</date>
		<username>ShootingShark</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Well-Made, Intense, Above Average Drama With Great Ensemble Cast</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's a chilly Thanksgiving weekend in 1974 in Connecticut, and the Hood family is having some problems. Dad's having an affair with a family friend, Mom's looking for something more than housewife drudgery and kids Paul and Wendy are trying to expand their sexual horizons. Is their unity splintering, and what force can bring them together again ?This is an impressive ensemble drama with an intriguing setting and a potent atmosphere of middle-class decadence. It's hard to like any of the adult characters in this picture, who belong to that interesting counter-culture-turned-parents generation who must surely win the award for most self-obsessed hypocrites, with their boring group therapy, indulgence in light narcotics, tedious wife-swapping parties and twisted double-standards towards their children. The kids on the other hand are all very sweet, despite their many flaws. The strength of the movie is the strength of the top-notch cast, all of whom are excellent, but particularly the underrated Allen as the timorous wife who holds the family together at the cost of her own individuality and the amazingly intense Hann-Byrd (the kid from Jodie Foster's Little Man Tate) as blank-faced boy Sandy, who can only get his parents' attention when he resorts to pyromania. Director Lee skilfully blends the characters and story threads together with a discreet, subtle style and the visual metaphor (a freezing rainstorm that covers everything in a layer of ice) richly embellishes the atmosphere of a family and a time that is superficially sparkling and pretty outwardly but cold and hard inside. The movie also has an odd comic-book connection in that narrator Maguire ascribes the events to the edition of The Fantastic Four he is reading; he went on to play Spiderman and Lee went on to direct The Hulk. Based on a book by Rick Moody and featuring a great unusual score by Mychael Danna.</fullReview>
	</review>

The Ice Storm is the better of the two films even if it doesn't have a performance that can top that of Kevin Spacey as Lester Bernham. The plot revolves around two families that live in a small superb of New York City in the mid seventies. One family consists of the sexually unhappy father who is having an affair with one of the neighbors played superbly by Kevin Kline, the happy but conservative wife played by Joan Allen, and their two kids played wonderfully by Tobey Maguire and Christina Ricci. The other family makes up of a father who is rarely there played by Jamey Sheridan, a mother who is wicked in her affairs and friendships and doesn't know how to let her kids know how much she loves them played by Sigourney Weaver and their two kids. The spacey teen who is dating Christina Ricci's character is played wonderfully by Elijah Wood and the quite nerdy younger brother played by Adam Hann-Byrd. The two families are put together in several incidents including a freak Ice Storm and a game of wife swap. All the characters are unhappy people but somehow even those of us that are most happy will find something they connect with. The Ice Storm is also a movie that everyone should see at some point in their life because it is amazing and very important.</fullReview>

I was caught in that storm. I was in my car,and on my to my Girlfriend's(she was 17 yrs. old)House.That Night,when I left my own home rain fell,butwhenI was driving on Interstate-95 the rain changed quickly to pounding ice pellets, and my car's defroster couldn't prevent all of my windows from freezing over. I also bought a can of WD40 from him to use in case the same problem came back on my journey. Upon my arrival,she told me that her parents had already left to go visit relatives (also in another town)and thus encountered the exact same driving conditions that I had going the other direction. Her Parents knew of our plans to go out that night,and had called her immediately upon their arrival to try and get ahold of me if I hadn't left to advise to not attempt the trip and stay home,but if I had already departed my own home, then we should both just stay put there and not attempt to travel anywhere. Thats exactly what we did. We started off watching "Columbo" on TV, but eventually ended up on the floor of her living room next to the family Christmas Tree making love. Thats the main part of my story,and thats where I relate to parallels in the lives of these fictional characters. By the time her parents managed to get back,we had straightened up enough,and I ended up sleeping on their couch that night.As for the rest of the movie,I had an Uncle then who reminded me a lot of the Kevin Klein character in this movie. He was then a 40 something successful businessman,who had been married to his wife for well over 20 years, with a nice home,family,and overall lifestyle,but,unknown to all of his family he was having an affair with a woman who was also married. His wife would eventually find out,divorce him,with the result being that he would lose his very nice home and many of it's nice furnishings. His long established business failed over the years,he drank a lot more,and would die from health complications due to his drinking mixed with diabetes. He did marry his girlfriend after she obtained her own divorce and they stayed together until she passed on a couple of years before him. Yes, the attitudes in this movie reflected a lot of what was really going out there in real-life in those days.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Over-the-top and contrived, but with four great young actors</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Ice Storm" is a film that isn't particularly special in any way. It's about a few middle-class families living in Connecticut, 1973. It doesn't really make clear what it's trying to say, but I assumed it was all about how middle-class families, despite their outside appearance, may not always be perfectly functional. (However, we all know that already and this doesn't contain much new material.) It trundles along with an average and slightly disjointed script, and portrays contrived and fairly unrealistic scenarios. The story is rushed sometimes, but then changes unpredictably to a slow pace. The cinematography is all right though, if you like looking at lots of shots of ice.The only real strength lies in the performances. Although the adult actors (lead by Joan Allen, Kevin Kline and Sigourney Weaver) are fine, they're nothing special. Fortunately, however, we have four younger actors: Tobey Maguire, Elijah Wood, Adam Hann-Byrd and Christina Ricci. Of these, unfortunately the only one with a decent amount of screen time is Christina Ricci, and her character is so annoying that her performances starts to become that way as well. Most of the other characters lacked character development. (It was interesting to see Tobey Maguire and Elijah Wood when they were younger than they were in their most well-known films; and great to see Adam Hann-Byrd who matured from "Little Man Tate".) The back of the video box informs me how everybody embarks upon their own "sexual odyssey" at Thanksgiving. That was an incredibly stupid way of describing the ridiculous and lame events that occur in the film. The ending is not satisfying and is almost rushed. Apart from the young actors, the end was my favourite part.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Might be the Darkest Movie I've Ever Seen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First the good, then the bad. Ang Lee is a terrific director in my book. CROUCHING TIGER, RIDE WITH THE DEVIL, and even HULK are excellent films in my book. He always has a great look to his films, and while not fast paced, are still well paced nonetheless. The cast he's assembled for THE ICE STORM is pretty amazing all by itself (including a pre-Spider-Man Tobey Maguire and and a pre-Frodo Elijah Wood). And Lee gets that cast, like all of his casts, to generate real people that I find believable on screen.But of all the movies of his I'd seen, nothing really prepared me for the kind of desperate realism portrayed here. I'm not sure any film I've watched played out as dark as this one. Sure, THE EXORCIST is dark, but there is something about those characters that are removed from me, not quite real or close to my reality. That and the horror aspect distanced me from it. TAXI DRIVER is closer to a reality I recognize, but again there are elements that seem removed and stylized, although that one hits pretty close these days. THE ICE STORM, however, gives such real and convincing performances that it's much more horrifying to see what 'normal' people - not demons, monsters, or stylized psychopaths - are capable of. Some of the characters here reminded me of some crazed train engineer who, upon realizing he's on the wrong tracks, just drives faster.So while Lee's film is involving, it never rewarded me in the way I think films have to. I just couldn't find anyone to root for here, and that was a frustrating experience (though not as frustrating as RAGING BULL - I'm sorry, I think it's Scorsese's worst effort, but that's another topic). It's hard for me to say if there's more bad than good to the movie, and my vote lands in the middle. People who loved MONSTER'S BALL or RAGING BULL might find this entertaining. But for most moviegoers, I'll recommend Ang Lee's much more balanced RIDE WITH THE DEVIL and say pass on this one.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-09-15</date>
		<username>David (davidals@msn.com)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Among The Greatest American Films Of The 90s</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*Possible Minor Spoilers*The post-war illusions of the 50s, the progress and disappointments of the 60s, post-hippie disillusionment, the positive and negative fallout from the sexual revolution, and the cold, reactionary paranoia of the Nixon years all collapsed - collectively - into the disorienting fragmentation/liberation of the 1970s. This process is captured hypnotically in Ang Lee's ICE STORM, which is basically a period piece, avoiding nostalgia (this film is every bit the doomy sibling of Richard Linklater's DAZED &amp; CONFUSED) in favor of a sociological/historic take on the era. The sterile anti-styles of the day (a pastiche of the groovy and the gauche - 50s modernism, 60s earthiness, 70s upward mobility) are flawlessly recreated and - along with the titular weather event - symbolize the characters' alternation between cerebral and chaotic responses to the new freedoms of the era that have inexplicably opened up before them. The adults (centered around two families) semi-secret sexual misadventures are pursued with a pathological coldness, and the similar fumblings (sex, booze and mysterious pills that Tobey Maguire heists from the medicine cabinet of his friend's parents) of their offspring seem more angry and compulsive than innocent. Within all of this darkness there is room for humor - when Kevin Kline's eldest son (Maguire) is introduced, he is seen in a boarding school dorm room sucking up bong hits as Frank Zappa's 'Dirty Love' plays in the background. Shortly thereafter, Kline attempts to deliver a 'birds-and-the-bees' speech to Maguire, which quickly degenerates into an explicit, yet rambling mess. But even the humor in on display here carries dire undercurrents of confusion and unarticulated rage - Elijah Wood blowing up his model airplanes before destroying a flower arrangement with a whip (these are not people who should have whips laying around the house), or a high school band slogging through a dissonant rendition of Maureen McGovern's already dreary radio staple 'There's Got To Be A Morning After' (music is very strategically deployed in this film, with song titles or lyrics very well used as discreet commentaries on the overall story). The impeccable look of THE ICE STORM, the accumulated weight of the characters' stunning neurosis and confusion, the music and cinematography all coalesce masterfully as the film builds toward a seemingly inevitable conclusion.</fullReview>
